   window.INITIAL_STATE = {"activities":{"items":{}},"ads":{"queries":{},"campaigns":{}},"annotations":{"items":{},"queries":{}},"app":{"authViewSource":"","authViewToShow":"","debug":false,"editorMenuToShow":"","hasUnsavedUploads":false,"isAnnotatingPhoto":false,"isAuthModalOpen":false,"isAuthPanelOpen":false,"isChromePanelOpen":false,"isCreateModalOpen":"","isDesktop":true,"isEditingArticle":false,"isEditingPhoto":false,"isEditorModalOpen":false,"isMenuOpen":false,"isMobile":false,"isPhotoCommentRepliesOpen":false,"isStartCollectionModalOpen":false,"isTablet":false,"isUnsavedStoryChanges":false,"isUploading":false,"nextPathname":"","preboardingActions":[],"previousPathnames":[],"replyCommentId":null,"settingsToShow":"","currentPathname":"\u002F"},"articles":{"items":{"6767685615473119232":{"type":"stories","id":"6767685615473119232","links":{"self":"https:\u002F\u002Fapi2.dwell.com\u002Fstories\u002F6767685615473119232"},"attributes":{"defaultImageId":"6764274390773641216","id":"6767685615473119232","lead":"Drawing on Japanese, Scandinavian, and Shaker influences, Whitten Architects designs a decidedly non-rustic lakeside retreat in New Hampshire.","title":"This Loon Lake Getaway Is a Wabi-Sabi Twist on the Traditional Cabin","storyType":"article","firstPublishedAt":"2021-03-19T20:11:07.000Z","layout":"traditional","body":"\u003Cp\u003EOftentimes, it’s the architect who encourages the client to embrace new ideas. But, when a New Hampshire couple approached Maine-based Whitten Architects about building a contemporary cabin on a quiet lake, they were the ones who brought the design concept to table. “We gave them a book for designers and philosophers about the wabi-sabi aesthetic, and they ran with it,” the wife says.&nbsp;\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274391731138560\" caption=\"The owners built the house as a place to gather with their three adult children on Loon Lake, where they had vacationed at the husband’s family home for years. The modern Adirondack chairs are by Loll Design.&nbsp;\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETom Lane, who designed the two-bedroom getaway, knew a bit about the Japanese aesthetic that focuses on appreciating the beauty of imperfection and impermanence. He was excited about the brief. “They have a sophisticated design vocabulary and a poetic interest in design,” Lane says. “That’s music to our ears.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274383470809088\" caption=\"Interior designer Heidi Lachapelle chose unfussy furnishings with clean lines. “Nothing should feel decorative or unnecessary,” she says. “We looked for things that would age beautifully to speak to the wabi-sabi concept.” The oak daybed is by Bautier, the indoor\u002Foutdoor rug is by Dash &amp; Albert, and the trapezoidal cushions on the concrete bench nod to similar ones that the wife saw at Georgia O’Keefe’s home and studio. The Scandinavian-inspired fireplace throws heat from two sides.\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe couple, who founded a non-profit contemplative retreat center called High Meadow Farm in Freedom, New Hampshire, were also inspired by adobe buildings—particularly artist Georgia O’Keefe’s adobe home and studio in Santa Fe. “I longed to echo those solid and earthy structures, but with a New England lake house flavor,” the wife says. “I envisioned the materiality of New England, and the Zen quality of adobe.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274389754425344\" caption=\"“Metaphorically, the cabin’s exterior is like a cut log,” Lane says. “The black-stained Western red cedar is the bark, and the Douglas fir siding under cover is the exposed wood once the log has been cut.” Beyond the house and native sod gardens, a meadowscape blends into the mature pine forest at the lakefront. “We wanted a woodland garden quality,” landscape architect Soren deNiord says.\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003ERather than mimic the traditional New England vernacular, which was imported from a milder climate, Lane found inspiration for the 1,500-square-foot dwelling in Scandinavian design. “The environment here is harsh, and the outside of the building has to deal with that,” he says. “Scandinavians do that well.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274382590005248\" caption=\"DiNiord collaborated with craftsman Ken Hood to design the concrete bench with firewood storage and detachable wood back. Douglas fir columns along the walkway creates a colonnade. The mono-sloped roof is a nod to the long roofline of the original house that stood on the property. “Reducing the angles also reflects the strictness to budget,” the wife says.&nbsp;\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274393420320768\" caption=\"“We designed the landscape and house in tandem to ensure continuity between the exterior and interior,” diNiord says. An outdoor sling chair by Croft House sits in front of the outdoor shower under the covered patio in front of the primary bedroom.\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThat concept paired well with another of the couple’s requests: that the design emphasize shadow as well as sunlight. The T-shaped floor plan boasts long, covered indoor\u002Foutdoor transitions that offer protection from snow and shade from sun.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274377768402944\" caption=\"A floor lamp nearly eight feet tall anchors the seating area in the living area. Ceilings that are 12 feet tall at the highest point help the room feel expansive. “We needed to find a way to define different areas in a relatively tight space,” Lachapelle says. It’s the clients’ first experience with an open floor plan. “We raised our kids in an old Victorian, and the farmhouse we live in now is chopped up into tiny rooms save for the studio we just added,” the husband says.\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn the living room, a series of windows and sliders look southeast to Loon Lake and let in loads of sunlight, while punched openings on the opposite side balance that with shadows and late afternoon light.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe glass corner by the fireplace welcome the landscape into the room, and sliders invite occupants to step outdoors. “The terrace is an extension of the living area,” says landscape architect Soren deNiord.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274390773641216\" caption=\"While the owners really liked the idea of shou sugi ban, they opted for a more cost-effective black stain. The random-width, reverse board-and-batten siding reflects the wabi-sabi concept. “The builder said the math for the random siding was torturous,” the wife said. “We didn’t know how hard it was to make things look simple.” DeNiord planted hay-scented fern and lowbush blueberry sod around the house. “We didn't want any side of the house to feel unconsidered,” he says. As for the local boulders he placed around the house and terrace, he says, “They give the feeling that the house grew up around the outcroppings.”\" layout=\"bleed\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274394625310720\" caption=\"DeNiord designed a simple concrete bench with a honed top to run parallel to the randomly sized concrete pads that lead to the covered entry. He planted blueberry bushes behind the bench and a river birch tree behind the boulder. To conjure a wabi-sabi feel outdoors, diNiord poured concrete around a boulder. “It represents the interruption of perfect geometry,” he says.\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EConcrete paving and outdoor furniture further meld the interior with the landscape. Poured-in-place concrete pads lead to the front entry and comprise the back courtyard, echoing the poured-in-place concrete floors throughout the home.\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274388930760704\" caption=\"In the entry, a high interior window borrows light from the bedroom wing and a woven wood screen in the kitchen lets the owners see who’s at the front door. Kalon Studios crafted the bench from a single ash log. “It will split a bit over time,” Lachapelle says.&nbsp;\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe owners relay a tale about how the installation of the floors resulted in an unintended but much appreciated wabi-sabi effect: “They poured the concrete in the fall, then covered it with foam sheets for the winter,” the husband says. “When they took them up in spring, the seams between the sheets created a large brickwork pattern on the concrete.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274377005039616\" caption=\"The wife notes that the pattern on the concrete reminds her of a floor she once saw in Nepal.&nbsp;\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe cool, earthy feel extends up the raw veneer plaster walls that harken back to the wife’s adobe dreams. She notes that the builder was initially hesitant about not painting it, for fear it would stain. “Once he saw how the light reflected on it, he understood,” she says. “Painted walls would have felt cold.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274378567434240\" caption=\"Modern Windsor chairs by Hay surround an oak extension table by Ethnicraft. “You flip a lever and it gets larger,” Lachapelle says. The original plan called for a center island, but the owners wanted the flexibility of a table they could also use as a prep surface. The oak cabinet behind the sofa is on casters for additional utility. “We can even use it outside,” the husband says.\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274383760203776\" caption=\"The tile backsplash matches the wall color, though it differs in texture, and the countertop is made from local granite. The light-colored wood screen set at the back of the darkly stained cabinetry mirrors the dark outside and light inside of the exterior facade.&nbsp; &nbsp;\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThey needn’t have worried, because the Douglas fir windows, ash cabinets stained a chameleon-like brownish black, and pale oak furniture ensure warmth. The use of each of the three species feels balanced. “Nothing should stand out more than anything else,” interior designer Heidi Lachapelle says.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274386835800064\" caption=\"“They’re well-traveled New Englanders who had always lived in older, traditional homes,” Lane says of the clients. “They wanted a contemporary home with warmth, texture, and craft.”\u003Cbr\u003E\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThat said, there is a (quiet) standout moment in the kitchen, albeit a practical one: a woven ash screen backs the open shelves. Dowels threaded through the slats hold them open just enough to let in light and allow someone standing at the sink to catch a glimpse of someone coming through the front entry. “It relates to the blonde brick at the fireplace,” the wife says, noting the similarity of rhythm and color.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274379830153216\" caption=\"There’s a one-foot level change down to the primary bedroom, which is Shaker in spirit. Instead of closets, each bedroom has a set of deep, built-in drawers. “The architects kept us to our word that the house is meant for short-term stays,” the husband says. Lachapelle sourced the Ethnicraft bed and side table from Fiore Home in Portland, Maine, and the vintage velvet Ligne Roset chair from the Brimfield Antique Market.\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Trent Bell\" photoUserId=\"6133535754879373312\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764274379068166144\" caption=\"In the primary bath, which includes the home’s only indoor shower, Lachapelle used plumbing fixtures and hardware with an oil-rubbed bronze finish that relate to the touches of black in the living space. Hardware","storyType":"article","firstPublishedAt":"2021-03-19T17:03:19.000Z","layout":"traditional","body":"\u003Cp\u003EHave you ever spotted someone wearing an impressive outfit—only to have the spell broken by poorly coordinated shoes? Or a dashing suit marred by an unfortunate tie? Like these fashion faux pas, a cabinet outfitted with dated or drab hardware can affect the overall impression of a room. Fortunately, switching out knobs, pulls, and handles is that rare type of home improvement project that’s easy, cost-effective, and won’t take longer than a weekend to complete.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6776292543144128512\" caption=\"Add a caption\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Mar Hernández\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhether you’re refreshing your \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.dwell.com\u002Farticle\u002Fmodern-cabinet-hardware-kitchen-c34ad2ed\"\u003Ekitchen cabinets\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, a bathroom vanity, or a \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.dwell.com\u002Farticle\u002Fbest-drawer-pulls-knobs-and-hardware-to-upgrade-your-dresser-e9748f46\"\u003Ebedroom dresser\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, the options are numerous. While there are no hard and fast rules when it comes to choosing the right hardware, a good place to start is calculating how many pieces you’ll need to replace, which will give you a ballpark spending figure. Knobs are cheaper and easier to install than pulls, but may not be as ergonomic—we advise using knobs for smaller drawers and pulls for wider designs. (That said, if your decor leans more toward a heritage style, two knobs on either end of a drawer might look more appropriate than a single, modern pull.)&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ECabinet hardware specialists suggest installing pulls that are approximately a third of the length of the drawer; extra long hardware that spans the entire length of the cabinet can look great in a modern setting. is to integrate intelligent technologies from day one—they deliver all of their homes with a standard smart tech package, which cuts down on the need for aftermarket gadgets. A wave of prefab manufacturers are at the forefront of this fundamental shift in home technology, which is ultimately driving us toward homes that are not only intelligent, but are also built smarter.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770019039069343744\" caption=\"Roombus is a new prefab home manufacturer building its Nest homes with its own RoomOS smart home platform.\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Roombus\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003E“These new prefab homes are higher performance homes, and the connected tech in them helps optimize that performance, whether it’s energy savings, water monitoring, or indoor air quality,” explains Sara Gutterman of \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgo.skimresources.com\u002F?id=96003X1550783&amp;isjs=1&amp;jv=15.1.0-stackpath&amp;sref=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.dwell.com%2Farticle%2F6770024781671043072%2Fedit&amp;url=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.greenbuildermedia.com%2Fblog%2Fauthor%2Fsara-gutterman&amp;xguid=01EZAPGZ37Q81Y1THN38H202F2&amp;xs=1&amp;xtz=420&amp;xuuid=3243821429275f3f5adeda3c25fe3608&amp;xjsf=other_click__contextmenu%20%5B0%5D\"\u003EGreen Builder Media\u003C\u002Fa\u003E, a company that partners with prefab builders to develop smart home concepts. “The housing sector is experiencing a major disruption, and prefab is on the leading edge. These companies are inclined to be more progressive and advanced in the way they are building homes.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAnother example is Dvele, a prefab home builder that has developed its own software to manage connected technologies in its homes. “DveleIQ was developed to deliver on three tenets: one, the health of the occupants; two, the health of the planet; and three, the health of the building,” says Matt Howland, president of Dvele.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe layering of smart and prefab technologies promises a more holistic approach to homebuilding, since it takes into account the entire life cycle of the home. “These are not going to be your least expensive options up front,” warns Gutterman, “but when you look at the full cost, you’re investing in technologies that are yielding zero utility bills, require less maintenance, and are more durable and resilient.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EHere, we look at five companies pushing the envelope when it comes to building smart from the start.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.dvele.com\u002F\"\u003EDvele\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770359660586577920\" caption=\"Dvele’s projects are built on the DveleIQ smart home platform, which uses artificial intelligence to optimize the health of the building and its occupants.\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Dvele\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWho they are\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESan Diego–based builder Dvele produces high-end modular homes built to Passive House standards. Their homes can go off-grid for up to four days, thanks to a full solar array and battery. Every home features their DveleIQ software platform, which is designed to provide automation as well as monitor and control building health and energy efficiency.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe company has a dozen different customizable designs, ranging from a 430-square-foot mini home starting at $165,000, to a four-bed, three-and-a-half-bath, 3,500-square-foot home that starts at $670,000. Dvele currently ships homes within a 1,000-mile radius of its factory near San Bernadino, California, and their prices don’t include shipping or home installation.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770019034080997376\" caption=\"Each of Dvele’s homes has over 300 sensors embedded in the walls to monitor temperature, humidity, air quality, and energy use. Pictured here is their Sedgemore model in Santa Rosa, California.&nbsp;\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Dvele\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhat they offer\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDvele’s homes are fully integrated smart homes. Each is equipped with over 300 Z-Wave sensors that relay real-time information about air quality, energy consumption, and home health to DveleIQ—an artificial intelligence software embedded on a hub in each home. DveleIQ interfaces with a number of well-known smart home brands—including Lutron lighting, Honeywell thermostats, Nuki locks, and Sonos audio—allowing everything to be controlled from a single app via your smartphone or iPad.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EUpgrades on offer include smart bulbs that can adapt to your circadian rhythm, home leak detection, a sensor package to monitor every door and window, and an enhanced audio\u002Fvisual system.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhy it’s smart\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDveleIQ is a learning platform designed specifically for its homes. It can use “Predictive Flows,” which means the&nbsp;home’s systems adapt to your everyday routines. “If you’re taking a shower every day between 7 a.m. and 7:15, we’ll actually start pushing the hot water recirculation button for you, so you'll automatically have hot water at the right time,” explains Howland. The system also constantly monitors air quality and can send a notification to you to turn on your hood fan if cooking is polluting the air. Or—if you allow it to—it can do it for you automatically.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThis is the next level of smart home—a home that is able to react to its inhabitants to improve the living environment. With environmental sensors that collect data, and a software platform to process that data, Dvele’s homes are embedded with a deeper layer of intelligence that goes beyond the capabilities of a series of connected gadgets.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.plantprefab.com\u002F\"\u003EPlant Prefab\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770019035002617856\" caption=\"LivingHome 9, a modernist redux of the classic American farmhouse, was designed by Plant Prefab’s in-house design studio. It features&nbsp;three bedrooms and two and a half baths in a svelte package.&nbsp;\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Plant Prefab\" photoUserId=\"6692184390211436544\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003E\u003Cspan style=\"font-family: Theinhardt, -apple-system, BlinkMacSystemFont, &quot;Segoe UI&quot;, Roboto, Oxygen-Sans, Ubuntu, Cantarell, &quot;Helvetica Neue&quot;, sans-serif; font-size: 24px;\"\u003EWho they are\u003C\u002Fspan\u003E\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EPlant Prefab LivingHomes are designed to be energy efficient, sustainable, and healthy for both the homeowners and the planet. They offer a range of designs from an impressive list of architects and firms that include Ray Kappe, Yves Béhar, Kieran Timberlake, and Koto. The styles range from a farmhouse-esque structure to a modernist townhouse. Additionally, Plant Prefab can custom design a home for you. All LivingHomes are solar capable.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770019035448340480\" caption=\"The interior of LivingHome 11, designed by Plant Design Studio for the Los Angeles City Small Lot Ordinance, can be built on a lot as small as 18 feet wide. Still, it offers 1,800 square feet of living space.&nbsp;\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Plant Prefab\" photoUserId=\"6692184390211436544\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhat they offer\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EPlant Prefab’s LivingHomes include the standard Living SmartHome package, which is centered around Amazon’s Alexa ecosystem. (Amazon invested in Plant Prefab in 2018.) Preinstalled in every LivingHomes home is an Eero Mesh Wi-Fi system that ensures connectivity throughout the home, an Amazon Echo Dot speaker, and a voice- and touch-controlled Echo Show 5 smart screen to manage home automation devices.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAn Ecobee smart thermostat manages the HVAC system using room sensors that measure temperature and occupancy, and a Lutron system keeps your lighting on task. A Ring video doorbell and Schlage Encode door lock work together to manage access to your home through one app, and a Sense energy monitoring system keeps track of your home’s energy use.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAn entertainment package upgrade includes Sonos speakers and an Amazon Fire TV Cube for voice controlling your television. A security package upgrade featuring a Ring Alarm and a Ring Stick Up Camera can be used indoors or out. “All the devices we select can integrate with each other, so you don't have to think about it,” says CEO Steve Glenn. And, once your home is in situ, Amazon will send out a technician to set everything up.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhy it’s smart\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EBalancing convenience, control, connectivity, and smart energy management, LivingHomes offer a comprehensive yet easy-to-use smart home package. Well-known brands interface smoothly with the Alexa smartphone app to provide a central place to manage the home, and the option to control everything with your voice.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAdditionally, the Sense energy monitor adds a deeper layer of connectivity and control, pairing with Energy Star appliances to help the homeowner manage and monitor the home’s energy use. “It shows you in real time what’s happening, and then also over time,\" says Glenn. “There’s been a lot of great research that shows that when people get feedback about their energy use, they tend to use less.”&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch4\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fconnect-homes.com\u002F\"\u003EConnect Homes\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh4\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6773258130888196096\" caption=\"Connect Homes has been building and installing prefab homes - such as this one in Los Angeles - for over a decade.\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Connect Homes\" photoUserId=\"6301124917338570752\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWho they are\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EFounded by \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.dwell.com\u002Farticle\u002Fqanda-with-connecthomes-co-founder-jared-levy-bb772187\"\u003EJared Levy\u003C\u002Fa\u003E and Gordon Stott in 2013 (both previously of Marmol Radziner Prefab—a company that pioneered the modern prefab movement), Connect Homes offers a streamlined approach to homebuilding. “We saw this huge demand to get modern, smart, sustainable homes, but they just had to be at a more affordable price,” says Levy. The company says its homes can be installed in two months and shipped anywhere in the world, thanks to a patented modular system that cuts transportation costs by up to 90 percent.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EConnect Homes’s 14 models range from 460-square-foot single-module homes to ten-module, 3,200-square-foot residences, and are priced between $174,00 and $825,000. But, unlike most prefab manufacturers, the pricing includes all design, manufacturing, transportation, and installation costs. Connect Homes allows you to tweak or upgrade their models, but they don’t allow custom designs or bespoke homes. “We’re trying to make it a lot like buying a car,” says Levy. They offer standard models, but you can move windows and doors around and fine tune with different finishes, fixtures, and technologies.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6773258206133735424\" caption=\"Connect Homes come with a selection of smart home features as standard, with the option to add upgrades such as smart motorized blinds, and a smart security system.\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Connect Homes\" photoUserId=\"6301124917338570752\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhat they offer\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAll Connect Homes come solar ready, and are pre-fitted with smart lighting, a zoned and ductless HVAC system, smart smoke alarms, and an energy recovery ventilation system. The Lutron Caseta lighting system includes dimmer switches and LED bulbs. A Lutron Bridge allows for voice control, phone control, and the ability to set scenes and routines. Nest Protect smoke alarms monitor smoke and carbon monoxide and can send alerts to you when you’re away from home.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EUpgrade options include Lutron Serena smart shades, a wireless Ring video doorbell, a Ring or Simplisafe security system with sensors and cameras, and a wireless Sonos home audio system. Homeowners can also add a water system by Moen that monitors for flooding; if a leak is detected, it automatically shuts off the water valve.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhy it's smart\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe company’s standard smart tech helps its homes be more efficient, adds convenience, enhances ambiance, and provides security and safety. “A well-executed smart home meets your needs, and also makes it more efficient and better to live in,” says Greg Leung, CEO of Connect Homes, and a former Apple executive. But the company’s advantage, according to Leung, is that its process is vertically integrated, and it has control of the entire homebuilding process. This allows them to “piece it all together in a way that’s coherent, and actually works together,” he says. “To date, the smart home has really underdelivered, and we think to deliver on the promise of the smart home you have to have great products that are properly installed and, on the software side, work together seamlessly.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fmethodhomes.net\u002F\"\u003EMethod Homes\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770380291503362048\" caption=\"Method Homes can design and install prefab homes of all sizes, and they can customize an existing design or create a custom one for you. Smart home options are similarly entirely customizable.&nbsp;\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Method Homes\" photoUserId=\"6133459922886418432\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWho they are\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMethod Homes is a Seattle-based luxury prefab manufacturer. Similar to Plant Prefab, they offer architect-designed models you can choose from and then customize. Or, you can work with them to design your own home.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThey offer LEED, Energy Star, Passive House, and net-zero energy homes, and all models are prewired for solar and can be delivered throughout the Western U.S. and Canada. Home styles range from 656-square-foot studios starting at $154,000, to a five-bed, five-and-a-half bath, 4,800-square-foot home with a garage and rooftop deck starting at $800,000. Those costs don’t include the design, engineering, permitting, or transportation of the home.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770380292624924672\" caption=\"Method Homes streamlines the installation of smart home technology by manufacturing parts in a controlled environment.&nbsp;\" layout=\"wide\" credit=\"Photo by Method Homes\" photoUserId=\"6133459922886418432\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhat they offer\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMethod Homes offers the latest in smart home technologies, but there is no standard package. Instead, you select add-ons a la carte. Typical options include smart lighting from Lutron and Noon, wireless audio, Nest thermostats, and smart, motorized window coverings. “One benefit of having a smart home is the ability to control all of your home from wherever is convenient,” says Brian Abramson, CEO and founder of Method Homes.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhy it’s smart\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EMethod Homes’s approach is to layer technology on top of the existing home’s systems, much like what you might do in a more traditionally built dwelling. However, it is all preinstalled and preconfigured to work together. Additionally, the company offers a number of different smart home systems, ultimately providing the buyer with more options. “We try to stay flexible and not get locked into any one system,” says Abramson. “This helps us keep up with the evolving technology and provide future flexibility for our customers.”\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch3\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fwww.roombus.com\u002F\"\u003ERoombus\u003C\u002Fa\u003E\u003C\u002Fh3\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770019038184976384\" caption=\"Roombus's all-in-one smart home—the Nest—comes in three sizes and has all the connected living features built into each unit. They’re powered by the company’s proprietary RoomOS smart home system.\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Roombus\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWho they are\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EA brand new company based in Los Angeles, Roombus is launching its first modular smart homes this summer. Called Nest, the disaster-proof homes resemble shipping containers, though the company’s CEO and founder, Dami Jegede, emphasizes that they aren’t. They are designed to be high-quality homes that are embedded from the beginning with Roombus’s RoomOS operating system.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThree models—240 square feet for $59,000, 336 square feet for $69,999, and 480 square feet for $89,000—can be configured in a variety of ways to suit your needs.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6770019038284660736\" caption=\"A Roombus kitchen with a control panel operates on the Roombus RoomOS smart home software.\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Photo by Roombus\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhat they offer\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003ERoombus takes an entirely different approach to the smart home, dispensing with name brands and instead building its own devices into the home. Electric locks are embedded into the door, sensors for humidity, temperature, and air quality are built into the walls, and HVAC controls are all digitally managed by RoomOS through either an iPad built into the home or with your smart phone. The whole building operates on a computer in the home, so it doesn’t rely on the cloud, and your data is stored locally.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Ch5\u003EWhy it’s smart\u003C\u002Fh5\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn addition to standard features such as smart lighting, remote control, and security, the home’s integrated systems can proactively help the homeowner. For example, it is able to monitor indoor air quality and automatically turn on a built-in air purifier, or if the outdoor air quality is good, bring in fresh air from outside and condition it to room temperature while you’re away. An integrated voice assistant—Roland—uses artificial intelligence to learn from your daily use and become more helpful over time.&nbsp;&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ci\u003ERelated Tantalus (Puʻu ʻŌhiʻa) from urban Honolulu—but it is a world away. Designated a forest preserve in the early 20th century, the range has been minimally developed. Thick with undergrowth and canopied by ironwood, guava, pine, and a variety of Hawaiian flowering trees, it gets 160 inches of rain per year compared to Waikiki’s 30.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EInterior designer Ginger Lunt knew the tight-knit neighborhood would be peaceful and beautiful, the ideal environment to live and to launch her design practice, aptly named Tantalus Studio.&nbsp;\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6765317706471514112\" caption=\"The approximately 200 homes that comprise the Tantalus neighborhood are not serviced by the municipal water or sewer system. These not only helped with the renovation, but gave Ginger a lot of “reverence and respect” for the existing design.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764967628716658688\" caption=\"The home was built on the only flat portion of the site; the rest of the property’s steep hillside allows for maximum views. &nbsp;\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Add credit\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6764968349130403840\" caption=\"The 1950s home featured local materials, including lava rock for its distinctive chimney and retaining wall.&nbsp;\" layout=\"\" credit=\"Add credit\" photoUserId=\"\"\u002F\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESubject to relentless rainfall and moisture, the home’s exterior had deteriorated and required substantial maintenance and retrofitting. The railing on the upper deck had to be completely redone, and the electrical systems were outdated. The floors, while in good condition, required refinishing, and the sliding doors and windows needed to be removed to be completely re-trimmed.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E“As with most older homes, the more you work on a home, the more problems you uncover,” notes Ginger.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cdwell-photo photoId=\"6767917175210741760\" caption=\"Before: The wear and tear of constant rainfall
